Title: Statistical Convergence of Spherical First Hitting Diffusion Models

URL Source: https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.07625v1

Published Time: Mon, 11 May 2026 01:06:43 GMT

Number of Pages: 48

Markdown Content:
# Statistical Convergence of Spherical First Hitting Diffusion Models 

Simon Bienewald вҲ— Lukas Trottner вҖ 

May 11, 2026 Denoising diffusion models have evolved into a state-of-the-art method for tasks in var-ious fields, such as denoising and generation of images, text generation, or generation of synthetic data for training of other machine learning models. First hitting diffusion models (FHDM) are a particular class of denoising diffusion models with random adaptive gener-ation time tailored to generate data on a known manifold. Building on the conditioning framework of DoobвҖҷs в„Һ-transform these models leverage the given information on the tar-get data manifold to demonstrate strong performance across tasks while offering distinct features such as time-homogeneous dynamics of the generating process and a reduced average simulation time. Even though the theoretical investigation of standard forward-backward diffusion models has attracted much attention in the recent past, the statistical convergence properties of FHDMs are not yet understood. In this work, we show that, up to logarithmic factors, FHDMs achieve the minimax optimal convergence rate in total vari-ation for spherically supported Sobolev smooth data distributions. In particular, this is the first statistical optimality result for denoising diffusion modelling with random generation time. 

1. Introduction 

Generative modelling has rapidly gained in importance in recent years, with applications including the generation of hyperrealistic images and videos, text and also synthetic data for training of large machine learning models. Many state of the art generative models are variants of score-based denoising diffusion models (DDMs) [27], which train neural networks to learn the drift of a stochastic diffusion process that is initialised in some easy-to-sample-from distribution and terminates in the targeted data distribution. The most widely used procedure simulates forward SDEs with Gaussian transition kernels initalised in the training data until approximate convergence to a prescribed Gaussian distribution to create a training data set of paths. Based on a denoising score matching procedure this data set is then used to learn the score of the forward process (the log -gradient of the forward marginal densities), which characterises the dynamics of the reverse (generating) process and contains the statistical information on the targeted unknown data distribution. Finally, new samples are generated as the terminal value of the approximated reverse SDE initialised in the approximate terminal distribution of the forward process. A drawback of denoising diffusion models compared to one-step sampling procedures such as GANs or variational autoencoders is the numerically expensive and highly sensitive generative procedure that requires many iterative sampling steps to approximate the reverse SDE on a fine discretised time-grid. Moreover, because of the time-inhomogeneous nature of the reverse process, the algorithm does not adapt to the noise level along generated paths since it relies on a fixed deterministic simulation 

> вҲ—University of Stuttgart, Institute for Stochastics and Applications, WankelstraГҹe 5, 70563 Stuttgart, Germany. Email: simon.bienewald@isa.uni-stuttgart.de
> вҖ University of Stuttgart, Institute for Stcohastics and Applications, WankelstraГҹe 5, 70563 Stuttgart, Germany. Email: lukas.trottner@isa.uni-stuttgart.de

1

> arXiv:2605.07625v1 [math.ST] 8 May 2026

time that needs to be sufficiently large for the forward process to terminate in a distribution that is approximately independent of the unknown data distribution. To address such issues when some prior geometric knowledge on the data support is given, [37] introduced First Hitting Diffusion Models (FHDMs). While FHDMs, similarly to DDMs, use an iterative SDE based sampling procedure, they do not rely on a time-reversal mechanism but aim to condition a simple SDE, such as a Brownian motion, to terminate in the target distribution О вҲ— at the stochastic first hitting time of the known data manifold. This is achieved by learning the log-gradient of a Doob 

в„Һ-transform [7] (the analogoue of the score in DDMs) that is fitted to О вҲ— via a denoising score matching procedure that involves drawing first hitting bridges to the given data at the training stage. For instance, FHDMs have been shown in [37] to produce high quality point clouds in в„қ3, climate data on the sphere, unweighted graphs, and image segmentations. Importantly, the authors demonstrate that leveraging the geometric information on the data in the design of the model leads to better generation performance compared to conventional DDMs, while the average (here random first-hitting) generation time can be significantly decreased. 

Our contribution 

To the best of our knoweldge, no theoretical analysis for FHDMs is available that can explain their im-pressive algorithmic performance. In this work, we therefore provide the first statistical convergence analysis of FHDMs for the particular case of data supported on the unit sphere рқң•рқҗө вҠӮ в„қрқ‘‘ in dimension 

рқ‘‘ вүҘ 3. Specifically, given i.i.d. data {рқ‘Ӣ 1, вҖҰ , рқ‘Ӣ рқ‘ӣ } with рқӣј -Sobolev smooth density ( рқӣј вҲҲ в„• вҲ© (( рқ‘‘ вҲ’ 1)/2 , вҲһ) )on the sphere that is uniformly bounded away from zero, we are interested in the total variation conver-gence rate of FHDMs in terms of the size рқ‘ӣ , dimension рқ‘‘ and smoothness рқӣј of the data. Approximating the score вҲҮ log в„Һ with sparse ReLU-neural network functions, our main result, Theorem 2.9, demon-strates that the output distribution МӮ О рқ‘ӣ of the learned model with an appropriate choice of рқ‘ӣ -dependent network sizes, converges at rate 

рқ”ј [TV(О  вҲ—,МӮ О рқ‘ӣ )] вүІ рқ‘ӣ вҲ’ рқӣј  

> 2рқӣј +рқ‘‘ вҲ’1

(log рқ‘ӣ )7/2 .

Since the sphere is (рқ‘‘ вҲ’ 1) -dimensional, this matches, up to small log -factors, the minimax optimal nonparametric rate for the data distributions under consideration. We thus prove statistical optimality of FHDMs, providing a theoretical explanation of their strong experimental performance. Moreover, this is the first statistical optimality result on generative modelling with adaptive random generation time. 

Related work 

Statistical theory for neural network based DDMs has made tremendous advances in recent years since the seminal work of [24], who were the first to prove statistical optimality in total variation of DDMs with OrnsteinвҖ“Uhlenbeck forward dynamics for Besov-smooth and lower bounded data densities on the cube [вҲ’1 , 1] рқ‘‘ . They also show that DDMs can implicitly adapt to the data geometry by proving almost optimal 1-Wasserstein convergence rates for data supported in a lower-dimensional linear subspace, see also [5]. The latter result was further refined in [31] and [3], where adaptivity of DDMs to more general low-dimensional manifold structures with almost optimal convergence rate in 1-Wasserstein distance is demonstrated, thus providing statistical evidence for the ability of DDMs to overcome the curse of dimensionality in light of the manifold hypothesis [11]. For other classes of data distributions with intrinsic dimensionality рқ‘‘ вҲ— smaller than the ambient dimension рқ‘‘ , [10, 17, 35] also demonstrate that the convergence rate of standard DDMs is only рқ‘‘ вҲ—-dependent. Notably, the results of [35] do not require lower-bound assumptions on the data distribution, which is an important feature that has also been demonstrated for full-dimensional supports in [38] for kernel-based score approximations and more recently in [28] for neural network approximations with tanh -activations. While all of the works above consider DDMs in their standard formulation with Gaussian transitions, some recent progress has also been made for statistical convergence of iterative generative models, which in a similar spirit 2to FHDMs, are designed to explicitly enforce geometric constraints on the data distribution such as [13, 14] for reflected diffusion models [21] and [34] for discrete diffusion models [22].

Structure of the paper 

In Section 2, we first introduce the necessary mathematical background on FHDMs, before introducing our assumptions on the target distribution in Section 2.1. After explaining and theoretically justifying the training and generation procedure of the model in Section 2.2, we present our formal main result in Section 2.3. Section 3 gives an overview of the central aspects of the proof of the main result and Section 4 discusses our results and their limitations. The appendix contains additional technical results as well as all proofs, with explicit pointers to their locations given in the main part.

Making (2.7) precise is technically delicate because of the pole of рқ‘һ (рқ‘Ҙ вҲЈ вӢ…) at рқ‘Ҙ . We will not need or attempt to formally prove this statement thanks to an early stopping procedure that we introduce for the generation algorithm and the statistical analysis. For our purposes it therefore suffices that (2.8) is a well-behaved SDE up to the first hitting-time of the boundary of any slightly smaller ball рқҗө 1вҲ’ рқңҖ for some рқңҖ вҲҲ (0 , 1) .The above discussion motivates a natural sampling procedure for the в„Һ-transform (2.3): we first sample рқ‘Ҙ from О вҲ— on рқң•рқҗө and then simulate a first hitting bridge рқ‘Қ рқ‘Ҙ according to (2.8). This procedure is of central importance for score-based generative modelling because the true score вҲҮ log в„Һ is implicitly defined via the unknown data distribution О вҲ— and is thus inaccesible in practice. The score therefore needs to be approximated by a learnable function рқ‘  based on paths of рқ‘Қ в„Һ, which given the data set

Lemma 2.1. It holds that 

в„ҷрқ‘§ (рқ‘Қ в„ҺрқңҸ в„Һ вҲҲ d рқ‘Ҙ ) = рқңӢ вҲ—(рқ‘Ҙ )

в„Һ(рқ‘§ ) в„ҡрқң•рқҗө (рқ‘§, dрқ‘Ҙ ) = рқңӢ вҲ—(рқ‘Ҙ )рқ‘һ (рқ‘Ҙ вҲЈ рқ‘§ )

в„Һ(рқ‘§ ) рқңҺ (d рқ‘Ҙ ), рқ‘§ вҲҲ рқҗө, рқ‘Ҙ вҲҲ рқң•рқҗө. 

In particular, в„ҷрқ‘§ 0 (рқ‘Қ в„ҺрқңҸ в„Һ вҲҲ d рқ‘Ҙ ) = О  вҲ—(d рқ‘Ҙ ).

Lemma 2.1 immediately yields the following denoising score representation. 

Lemma 2.2. 

вҲҮ log в„Һ(рқ‘§ ) = рқ”ј рқ‘§ [вҲҮ2 log рқ‘һ (рқ‘Қ в„ҺрқңҸ в„Һ вҲЈ рқ‘Қ в„Һ

The proofs of both lemmata are given in Appendix B. 

2.1. Assumptions on the target distribution О вҲ—

Our goal in this paper is to prove optimal nonparametric convergence rates of spherical first hitting diffusion models under Sobolev regularity assumptions on the target distribution О вҲ—. To this end, we first introduce the necessary background on (Riemannian) analysis on the sphere and then state our regularity assumptions on the data density рқңӢ вҲ—.The space of square-integrable functions on рқң•рқҗө wrt the surface measure рқңҺ is defined by 

рқҗҝ 2(рқң•рқҗө ) вү”

{

рқ‘ў вҲ¶ рқң•рқҗө вҶ’ в„қ вҲ¶ вҲ«рқң•рқҗө 

|рқ‘ў |2 dрқңҺ < вҲһ

}

,

which we equip with the inner product 

вҹЁрқ‘ў, рқ‘Ј вҹ©рқҗҝ 2(рқң•рқҗө ) вү” вҲ«рқң•рқҗө 

рқ‘ўрқ‘Ј dрқңҺ, рқ‘ў, рқ‘Ј вҲҲ рқҗҝ 2(рқң•рқҗө ).

For notational simplicity we will omit the subscript of the scalar product as long as there is no risk of confusion with the usual scalar product on рқҗҝ 2(рқҗө ).5Definition 2.3 (LaplaceвҖ“Beltrami operator) . For a differentiable function рқ‘ў , the LaplaceвҖ“Beltrami oper-ator on the unit sphere can be defined by [29] 

вҲ’О” рқң•рқҗө рқ‘ў (рқ‘Ҙ ) вү” вҲ’О” рқ‘§ рқ‘ў (рқ‘§ /вҖ–рқ‘§ вҖ–)|рқ‘§ =рқ‘Ҙ , рқ‘Ҙ вҲҲ рқң•рқҗө,

рқ‘ў вҲҲ Dom(О” рқң•рқҗө ) вү” {рқ‘ў вҲҲ рқҗҝ 2(рқң•рқҗө ) вҲ¶ в„қрқ‘‘ вҲӢ рқ‘§ вҶҰ рқ‘ў (рқ‘§ /вҖ–рқ‘§ вҖ–) is twice weakly differentiable }.

The LaplaceвҖ“Beltrami operator is a self-adjoint operator wrt the inner product on the sphere, with eigenvalues рқ‘ҷ (рқ‘ҷ +рқ‘‘ вҲ’2) , рқ‘ҷ вҲҲ в„•, and corresponding orthonormal eigenfunctions рқ‘Ң рқ‘ҷрқ‘ҡ , рқ‘ҡ = 1 , вҖҰ , рқ‘Җ рқ‘ҷ , where 

рқ‘Җ рқ‘ҷ denotes the dimension of the eigenspace to the рқ‘ҷ th eigenvalue. The рқ‘Ң рқ‘ҷрқ‘ҡ are the so-called spherical harmonics and are well-known from harmonic analysis, Riemannian geometry and representation the-ory. Due to the self-adjointness of О”рқң•рқҗө , they build a complete basis of рқҗҝ 2(рқң•рқҗө ). We are now ready to introduce the space of Sobolev functions on the sphere. In our context, it will be most convenient to use the following characterisation. 

Definition 2.4 (Sobolev space on the sphere) . For рқӣј вҲҲ в„•, the Sobolev space on the (unit) sphere is given by

This will allow us to easily relate the Sobolev smoothness of the score function вҲҮ log в„Һ to the Sobolev smoothness of the data density рқңӢ вҲ—, thus determining the speed of convergence of the neural network approximation. With this preparation, we can now introduce the following assumptions on the target distribution, which we assume to hold for the remainder of the paper without further mention. (H1) рқңӢ вҲ— вҲҲ рқҗ» рқӣј (рқң•рқҗө ) for рқӣј вҲҲ в„• and рқӣј > (рқ‘‘ вҲ’ 1)/2 вүҘ 1.(H2) рқңӢ вҲ— is uniformly bounded away from zero, that is, there exists a constant рқңӢ min > 0 such that 

рқңӢ min вүӨ рқңӢ вҲ—.According to the Sobolev embedding theorem for manifolds [1, Theorem 2.20], the Sobolev-smoothness assumption ( H1) implies that рқңӢ вҲ— is continuous and thus bounded from above as well. The lower bound-edness assumption ( H2) on the data distribution is typical in the literature on statistical convergence rates of diffusion models [3, 14, 24, 31] and simplifies the approximation analysis of the score function 

вҲҮ log в„Һ = вҲҮ в„Һ/в„Һ considerably since it implies that в„Һ = вҲ«рқң•рқҗө рқңӢ вҲ—(рқ‘Ҙ ) в„ҡрқң•рқҗө (вӢ…, dрқ‘Ҙ ) вүҘ рқңӢ min > 0. Consequently, we only consider target distributions О вҲ— with full support on the unit sphere рқң•рқҗө .

2.2. Generation and estimation strategy 

Similarly to forward-backward diffusion models with fixed time horizon, the main idea to approximate the unknown score вҲҮ log в„Һ is to use GirsanovвҖҷs theorem [18, Theorem 5.22] to first express the KL divergence between the path measures induced by the approximating drift рқ‘  and the вҖңtrueвҖқ drift вҲҮ log в„Һ

in terms of an рқҗҝ 2-loss along the paths рқ‘Ў вҶҰ рқ‘Қ в„Һрқ‘Ў . By establishing an equivalence of this explicit score loss to a denoising score loss that does not involve the unknown score вҲҮ log в„Һ but only known transition kernels (in our case simply the Poisson kernel рқ‘һ (рқ‘Ҙ вҲЈ рқ‘§ ) thanks to the time-homogeneous nature of the generating process), a training objective is obtained, which (with a further Monte-Carlo approximation step) can be optimised, usually over a class of neural networks. However, вҖ–вҲҮ log в„ҺвҖ– cannot be guaranteed to be bounded in a neighbourhood of рқң•рқҗө , making the ap-plicability of GirsanovвҖҷs theorem and finiteness of the explicit score matching loss a delicate issue. To 6circumvent such problems, we do not aim at simulating рқ‘Қ в„Һ until its terminal time рқңҸ в„Һ, but stop a little early at first hitting of the (1 вҲ’ рқңҖ )-sphere рқң•рқҗө 1вҲ’ рқңҖ for small рқңҖ > 0. To generate a data distribution with the correct support, the simulated value of the early stopped process on рқң•рқҗө 1вҲ’ рқңҖ is then simply projected onto рқң•рқҗө .Let us therefore introduce рқңҸ 1вҲ’ рқңҖ (рқң” ) вү” inf{ рқ‘Ў вүҘ 0 вҲ¶ рқң” рқ‘Ў вҲҲ рқң•рқҗө 1вҲ’ рқңҖ } for рқң” вҲҲ в„қ[0 ,вҲһ) and set рқңҸ в„Һ

With this preparation we are now in a position to state the full generative algorithm given in Algo-rithm 1. 

Remark 2.8 . (i) Implicitly, the generative algorithm uses the simplifying assumption that we can evaluate the expectation рқҗҝ рқ‘  (рқ‘Ҙ ) and that for an obtained score estimator МӮрқ‘  we can exactly simulate the corresponding absorbed SDE. In practice, рқҗҝ рқ‘  (рқ‘Ҙ ) needs to be numerically approximated via a Monte-Carlo estimator based on simulated paths of рқ‘Қ рқ‘Ҙ . A fast simulation procedure for рқ‘Қ рқ‘Ҙ with initialisation рқ‘§ 0 = 0 that is based on simple rotations of simulated paths of the unconditional stopped Brownian motion to the prescribed exit location рқ‘Ҙ вҲҲ рқң•рқҗө is given in [37, Proposition 2.11]. Analysing the numerical convergence rate of such a Monte-Carlo estimator is technically challenging because of the path-dependent random upper limit of the path integral and out of scope of this work. The statistical analysis of the generative model taking into account sampling effects of both the training and generative procedure are therefore left to future work. 8Algorithm 1 Generative algorithm

until its first hitting time МӮрқңҸ 1вҲ’ рқңҖ of the (1 вҲ’ рқңҖ )-sphere рқң•рқҗө 1вҲ’ рқңҖ .

Output: projected value рқ‘ғ рқң•рқҗө рқ‘Қ МӮрқ‘ МӮ рқңҸ 1вҲ’ рқңҖ as new (approximate) sample for О вҲ—.(ii) Imposing uniform growth restrictions on the neural networks from the approximation class S is a typical feature in statistical analysis of score-based generative models. For diffusion models with deterministic sampling horizon the growth is controlled in time [3, 13, 14, 24, 31, 35] to match the explosive behaviour of the score close to termination of the algorithm. In our case, the score has no time component, but the distance to the sphere as our target manifold can be regarded as an intrinsic time-scale of the algorithm since it is proportional to the average time left until termination and controls the gowth of the pulling drift вҲҮ log в„Һ of the generating process. Our spatial growth restriction therefore serves as a natural analogue to estimation strategies in time-inhomogeneous models. Technically, such a condition is needed to control the covering number and the uniform bound of the class of loss functions {рқҗҝ рқ‘  вҲ¶ рқ‘  вҲҲ S}, which crucially determine the convergence rate of МӮрқ‘  . The constant in the nominator is chosen to make the handling of constants simpler in the proof, but could, in principle, be chosen as an arbitrarily large number greater than 

рқ‘‘ + 2 .

2.3. Main result 

Our main result is the following. 

Theorem 2.9. Let рқңҖ = рқ‘ӣ вҲ’рқӣј /( рқӣҪ (2 рқӣј +рқ‘‘ вҲ’1)) for рқӣҪ = ( рқӣј вҲ’ ( рқ‘‘ вҲ’ 1)/2) вҲ§ 1 . Then for О вҲ— satisfying assumptions (H1) and ( H2), there exist neural network size parameters of order

such that Algorithm 1 with S chosen as in (2.16) produces an output рқ‘ғ рқң•рқҗө рқ‘Қ МӮрқ‘ МӮ рқңҸ 1вҲ’ рқңҖ such that

Our proof shows that the same result, but with a smaller logarithmic term O(log 3 рқ‘ӣ ), holds true if the neural networks take spherical coordinates as input. By the following proposition, Theorem 2.9 demonstrates that up to log-factors, the convergence rate of FHDMs matches the minimax lower bound for total variation density estimation for spherical data.

The general proof of Theorem 2.9 follows a similar path to previous statistical work on diffusion mod-els. However, the technical details are of a fundamentally different nature and impose new challenges because of the stochastic termination criterion, which places the analytic framework quite naturally into potential theory for Markov processes [4, 7]. Before moving to the detailed intermediate results that the proof of Theorem 2.9 builds upon in the next section, we give a short breakdown of the proof below. In a first step, we control the early stopping error in (2.9) in Proposition 3.1, where we show that the total variation distance between the target distribution О вҲ— and the projected distribution of the в„Һ-transform stopped in рқң•рқҗө 1вҲ’ рқңҖ is at most of order рқңҖ рқӣҪ /2 , where we recall that рқӣҪ is the minimally guaranteed HГ¶lder smoothness of the target density рқңӢ вҲ—. This allows us to focus on the second error component in (2.9), which is the generation error on the slightly smaller sphere рқң•рқҗө 1вҲ’ рқңҖ . By Proposition 2.5 and PinskerвҖҷs inequality, this error is characterised by the expected explicit score matching loss (2.10) of the empirical risk minimiser МӮрқ‘  . Given the equivalence of explicit and denoising score matching loss (Proposition 2.6), which is also the central component for the statistical analysis of denoising diffusion models [3, 13, 14, 24, 32, 35], this can be decomposed into an approximation error and an error influenced by the complexity of the class L = { рқҗҝ рқ‘  вҲ¶ рқ‘  вҲҲ S} of denoising score losses, cf. Theorem 3.2, which need to be balanced via an appropriate selection of the network parameters to achieve the optimal rate. The complexity term is determined by the covering number of L = { рқҗҝ рқ‘  вҲ¶ рқ‘  вҲҲ S} built from S and the size of the uniform control рқҗ¶ (L) = sup рқ‘  вҲҲS вҖ–рқҗҝ рқ‘  вҖ–рқҗҝ вҲһ(рқң•рқҗө ). These components are controlled via Lemma 3.3 in terms of the covering number of the approximation class S and Proposition 3.4, respectively, exploiting the growth rate of the neural networks and the score вҲҮ log в„Һ near рқң•рқҗө . On the other hand, the approxima-tion error of the neural network class is carried out in three main steps: first, the score is approximated on the (рқ‘‘ вҲ’ 1) -dimensional coordinate space, yielding convergence rates depending on the manifold dimension (рқ‘‘ вҲ’ 1) instead of the ambient dimension рқ‘‘ . Then, the coordinate map рқң‘ рқң•рқҗө вҲ¶ рқң•рқҗө вҶ’ в„қрқ‘‘ вҲ’1 

is approximated, which is cheap in terms of neural network size due to the arbitrary smoothness of 

рқң‘ рқң•рқҗө . Finally, the two constructed neural networks are concatenated to approximate the score on the ambient space. This yields a parsimonious choice for sufficient network sizes that allows (up to log factors) approximation at rate O(рқ‘ӣ вҲ’рқӣј /(2 рқӣј +рқ‘‘ вҲ’1) ), while keeping the complexity term at the same order, thus finishing the proof.

We start with the error contribution by stopping the generation procedure early on рқң•рқҗө 1вҲ’ рқңҖ and then projecting onto the sphere рқң•рқҗө . This is described by the the first term in (2.9) and can be bounded as follows in terms of the early stopping parameter рқңҖ and the minimally guaranteed HГ¶lder smoothness 

рқӣҪ of the data density рқңӢ вҲ—.

for a constant рқҗ¶ independent of рқңҖ .

We split the spatial integral into two sub-domains motivated by the asymptotic behaviour of the Green kernel: a fixed interior ball, where the Green kernel is singular, but the integrand is uniformly bounded, and the remaining annulus, where the Green kernel рқҗә 1вҲ’ рқңҖ (рқ‘§, рқ‘§ 0) decreases linearly to 0 with the distance of рқ‘§ to the boundary, cf. Lemma F.3, thus limiting the average time spent by the Brownian motion close to the sphere before being absorbed. This allows to appropriately downweigh the explosive behavior of the score вҲҮ log в„Һ in this critical area for approximation purposes.

Choosing рқӣҫ вү” рқ‘‘ рқ‘‘ вҲ’1 рқӣј ,which is valid for рқӣј > (рқ‘‘ вҲ’ 1)/2 , and parallelising the networks obtained from Lemma 3.7, then yields the desired network. This is summarised in the following proposition. 

Proposition 3.8. For рқ‘Ғ вҲҲ в„•, there exists a neural network рқ‘  вҲҲ NN( L, W, S, B) with 

L вүІ log рқ‘Ғ , W вүІ рқ‘Ғ , S вүІ рқ‘Ғ log рқ‘Ғ , B = Poly( рқ‘Ғ ),

such that 

вҖ–вҲҮ log в„Һ вҲ’ рқ‘  вҖ–рқҗҝ вҲһ(рқҗө рқ‘… ) вүІ рқ‘Ғ вҲ’рқӣј /( рқ‘‘ вҲ’1) .

This proposition already bounds the second term in (3.3). The remaining approximation on the annulus рқҗө 1вҲ’ рқңҖ в§ө рқҗө рқ‘… is much more involved. 

рқҗҝ 2 approximation on рқҗө 1вҲ’ рқңҖ в§ө рқҗө рқ‘… The approximation of the score on рқҗө 1вҲ’ рқңҖ requires a more careful con-struction of a neural network, since the Sobolev norm of вҲҮ log в„Һ diverges as рқңҖ decreases to zero. Our approximation strategy is inspired by the approach from [14] for neural network approximation of space-time functions, where in our setting the radial coordinate plays the rГҙle of the time variable. The approximation strategy can be summarised as follows: 1. We start with approximating в„Һ and вҲҮв„Һ by functions в„Һрқ‘Ғ and вҲҮв„Һрқ‘Ғ , which are finite sums of mono-mials in the radial coordinate multiplied with рқҗҝ 2-basis functions on the (рқ‘‘ вҲ’ 1) -dimensional unit sphere. 2. For fixed radii рқ‘ҹ рқ‘– we then construct spherical neural network approximations of в„Һрқ‘Ғ (рқ‘ҹ рқ‘– , вӢ…) and 

вҲҮв„Һрқ‘Ғ (рқ‘ҹ рқ‘– , вӢ…), exploiting the induced smoothness from our target distribution рқңӢ вҲ—.3. The neural network approximations for fixed radii are then combined via polynomial interpola-tion in рқ‘ҹ to obtain neural network approximations of (рқ‘ҹ, рқ‘Ҙ ) вҶҰ в„Һрқ‘Ғ (рқ‘ҹ, рқ‘Ҙ ) and (рқ‘ҹ, рқ‘Ҙ ) вҶҰ вҲҮв„Һрқ‘Ғ (рқ‘ҹ, рқ‘Ҙ ),which are in turn approximated by neural networks. Because the finite approximations в„Һрқ‘Ғ and 

вҲҮв„Һрқ‘Ғ are entire functions in рқ‘ҹ [33], it suffices to choose a number of radii that scale logarithmically in рқ‘Ғ to achieve an optimal convergence rate, while keeping a O(log рқ‘ӣ ) dependence of all network hyperparameters L, W, S, B.4. At the end of this procedure, we get neural network approximations рқ‘  в„Һрқ‘Ғ в—Ұрқң‘ вҲ’1 Вұ and рқ‘  вҲҮв„Һрқ‘Ғ в—Ұрқң‘ вҲ’1 Вұ of в„Һрқ‘Ғ and 

вҲҮв„Һрқ‘Ғ in spherical coordinates, i.e., of the functions в„Һ в—Ұ рқң‘ вҲ’1 Вұ and вҲҮв„Һ в—Ұ рқң‘ вҲ’1 Вұ , where рқң‘ Вұ are stereograph-ical projection maps. In a final step, we concatenate these with neural network approximations 

рқ‘  рқң‘ Вұ of рқң‘ Вұ to get approximations of в„Һрқ‘Ғ and вҲҮв„Һрқ‘Ғ in cartesian space. 5. In a final step, the score вҲҮ log в„Һ = вҲҮ в„Һ/в„Һ вүҲ вҲҮ в„Һрқ‘Ғ /в„Һрқ‘Ғ is approximated by a neural network approx-imation of the quotient of the neural network approximations of в„Һрқ‘Ғ and вҲҮв„Һрқ‘Ғ .The function в„Һ and its spherical representation Мғ в„Һ вҲ¶ (0 , 1) Г— рқң•рқҗө вҶ’ в„қ of в„Һ are uniquely related by 

в„Һ(рқ‘§ ) = Мғ в„Һ(вҖ–рқ‘§ вҖ–, рқ‘§ /вҖ–рқ‘§ вҖ–), рқ‘§ вҲҲ рқҗө в§ө {0} . To not overburden notation, we will not distinguish between both representations notationally, that is, we identify в„Һ =Мғ в„Һ. The same convention will be used for вҲҮв„Һ and for the approximations в„Һрқ‘Ғ and вҲҮв„Һрқ‘Ғ . In order to approximate в„Һ by a polynomial in the radial coordinate, we note that the Poisson kernel can be represented in terms of the spherical harmonics рқ‘Ң рқ‘ҷрқ‘ҡ by

4. Conclusion 

In this paper, we gave the first statistical optimality results for iterative generative models with ran-dom termination criterion by showing that FHDMs achieve the minimax optimal convergence rate 

O(рқ‘ӣ вҲ’рқӣј /(2 рқӣј +рқ‘‘ вҲ’1) ) up to logarithmic factors for spherical data with рқӣј -Sobolev smooth and uniformly lower bounded density рқңӢ вҲ—, where рқӣј вҲҲ (( рқ‘‘ вҲ’ 1)/2 , вҲһ) вҲ© в„•. Given the stochastic termination criterion and the time-homogeneous nature of the algorithm, both the probabilistic as well as the score approximation analysis imposed several new challenges compared to the analysis of denoising diffusion models with deterministic time horizon, which we solved based on arguments rooted in general Markov processes theory and spherical Riemannian geometry. To finish the paper, let us comment on limitations that arise from our setting and the made assump-tions. The restriction on рқӣј implies that the density must be HГ¶lder smooth and it is a technically challenging question how to extend this to a non-smooth setting, where we have no access to Sobolev embeddings, similarly to [24] and their general Besov smoothness framework in the context of DDMs. The lower boundedness assumption on рқңӢ вҲ—, albeit a common assumption in the field of statistics for 15 generative models, also imposes a notable restriction on the generality of the statistical setting and is highly non-trivial to overcome. A further natural question that arises from this paper is how the con-vergence analysis of FHDMs may be extended to more general target manifolds M than the sphere. The general score matching results from section 2 and the general risk decomposition extend without any problems, where a suitable early stopping criterion becomes the first hitting time of an рқңҖ -environment of M. Moreover, [16] shows that the Poisson kernel for more general smooth domains is asymp-totically comparable to the Poisson kernel for the sphere near the boundary, which allows to extend some technical arguments in this paper that are based on the explicit form of the Poisson kernel. A direct extension of our score approximation strategy, however, critically depends on the geometry and smoothness of M, with the availability of a suitable expansion of the Poisson kernel being a central feature of our analysis. It should however also be noted that FHDMs are specifically designed for data supports admitting an easily implementable Poisson kernel such that highly complicated or even un-known domains require a different ansatz for random adaptive termination rules, see e.g. [6]. Finally, we emphasise that we havenвҖҷt considered discrete sampling effects for both the training as well as the generation procedure as discussed in Section 2.2, which given the stochastic termination criterion is an interesting question from a numerical point of view for future work. 

A. Technical results on hitting times and в„Һ-transforms 

Lemma A.1. Let рқ‘Қ be the strong solution of

рқңҲ is concentrated on рқҗө and рқ‘Ҡ is a рқ‘ӣ -dimensional Brownian motion indpendent of рқ‘Қ 0. Denote by рқңҸ the first exit time of рқ‘Қ out of рқҗө . Then

рқҗҙрқ‘ў рқ‘ҳ = вҲ’ рқ‘ҳрқ‘ў рқ‘ҳ вҲ’1 on рқҗө, рқ‘ў рқ‘ҳ = 0 on рқң•рқҗө, 

where рқҗҙ is the infinitesimal generator of рқ‘Қ . We show that solutions in рқҗ¶ 2(рқҗө ) exist for all рқ‘ҳ вҲҲ в„•

by induction. Since both рқ‘ў 0 and the diffusion coefficients are in рқҗ¶ рқӣҫ (рқҗө ) for any рқӣҫ < 1, the solution 

рқ‘ў 1 вҲҲ рқҗ¶ 2+ рқӣҫ (рқҗө ) exists by [12, Corollary 6.9.]. Thus, for fixed рқ‘ҳ вҲҲ в„•, рқ‘ў рқ‘ҳ вҲ’1 вҲҲ рқҗ¶ рқӣҫ (рқҗө ) and so рқ‘ў рқ‘ҳ вҲҲ рқҗ¶ 2+ рқӣҫ (рқҗө )

exists. Using that рқҗө is compact and рқ‘ў рқ‘ҳ is continuous, we get sup рқ‘Ҙ вҲҲрқҗө рқ”ј [рқңҸ рқ‘ҳ вҲЈ рқ‘Қ 0 = рқ‘Ҙ ] < вҲһ and finally 

рқ”ј [рқңҸ рқ‘ҳ ] = вҲ«рқҗө 

рқ”ј [рқңҸ рқ‘ҳ вҲЈ рқ‘Қ 0 = рқ‘Ҙ ] рқңӮ (d рқ‘Ҙ ) < вҲһ.

в– 

Lemma A.2. For any рқ‘§ вҲҲ рқҗө 1вҲ’ рқңҖ it holds that рқ”ј рқ‘§ [вҖ–рқ‘Ҡ рқңҸ вҲ’ рқ‘Ҡ рқңҸ 1вҲ’ рқңҖ вҖ–] вүӨ вҲҡрқңҖ (2 вҲ’ рқңҖ ).Proof. By JensenвҖҷs inequality and the strong Markov property of Brownian motion [7, Chapter 2.3, Theorem 3], it follows that 

рқ”ј рқ‘§ [вҖ–рқ‘Ҡ рқңҸ вҲ’ рқ‘Ҡ рқңҸ 1вҲ’ рқңҖ вҖ–] вүӨ (рқ”ј рқ‘§ [вҖ–рқ‘Ҡ рқңҸ вҲ’ рқ‘Ҡ рқңҸ 1вҲ’ рқңҖ вҖ–2])

> 12

= рқ”ј рқ‘§ [рқ”ј рқ‘Ҡ рқңҸ 1вҲ’ рқңҖ [вҖ–рқ‘Ҡ рқңҸ вҲ’ рқ‘Ҡ 0вҖ–2]]1/2 (A.2) 16 where we used for the second line that рқңҸ 1вҲ’ рқңҖ вүӨ рқңҸ under в„ҷрқ‘§ since рқ‘§ вҲҲ рқҗө 1вҲ’ рқңҖ . For any рқ‘Ҙ вҲҲ рқҗө we have 

рқ”ј рқ‘Ҙ [вҖ–рқ‘Ҡ 0вҖ–2 вҲ’ рқ‘‘ Г— 0] = рқ”ј рқ‘Ҙ [вҖ–рқ‘Ҡ рқңҸ вҖ–2 вҲ’ рқ‘‘рқңҸ ] by the optional stopping theorem and thus 

вҖ–рқ‘Ҙ вҖ–2 = рқ”ј рқ‘Ҙ [вҖ–рқ‘Ҡ рқңҸ вҖ–2 вҲ’ рқ‘‘рқңҸ ] = 1 вҲ’ рқ‘‘ рқ”ј рқ‘Ҙ [рқңҸ ].

Similarly, optional stopping for the mean zero martingale вҖ–рқ‘Ҡ рқ‘Ў вҲ’ рқ‘Ҡ 0вҖ–2 вҲ’ рқ‘‘рқ‘Ў under в„ҷрқ‘Ҙ yields 

рқ”ј рқ‘Ҙ [вҖ–рқ‘Ҡ рқңҸ вҲ’ рқ‘Ҡ 0вҖ–2 вҲ’ рқ‘‘рқңҸ ] = 0 .

Combining the above gives рқ”ј рқ‘Ҙ [вҖ–рқ‘Ҡ рқңҸ вҲ’ рқ‘Ҡ 0вҖ–2] = 1 вҲ’ вҖ–рқ‘Ҙ вҖ–2, which inserted into (A.2) finally yields 

рқ”ј рқ‘§ [вҖ–рқ‘Ҡ рқңҸ вҲ’ рқ‘Ҡ рқңҸ 1вҲ’ рқңҖ вҖ–] вүӨ рқ”ј рқ‘§ [1 вҲ’ вҖ–рқ‘Ҡ рқңҸ 1вҲ’ рқңҖ вҖ–2]1/2 = [1 вҲ’ (1 вҲ’ рқңҖ )2]1/2 = вҲҡрқңҖ (2 вҲ’ рқңҖ )

C. Proof of the early stopping bound 

Proof of Proposition 3.1. The proof uses ScheffГ©вҖҷs lemma to relate the total variation distance to the рқҗҝ 1-distance of the densities wrt some reference measure, which we choose as the distribution в„ҷрқ‘§ 0 (рқ‘Ҡ рқңҸ вҲҲ вӢ…).23 While the density of О вҲ— is рқңӢ вҲ— by definition, the density of рқ‘ғ рқң•рқҗө вҷҜв„ҡв„Һрқң•рқҗө 1вҲ’ рқңҖ is not directly obvious. The stereographic coordinate system has the advantage, compared to standard spherical coordinates, that it does not involve trigonometric functions and the determinant of its metric, which appears in surface integrals, is strictly bounded from below. This will come in handy, when approximating рқң‘ +

Thus, the size of the parallelization (рқ‘қ в—Ұ рқ‘  рқ‘ҹ 2 , рқ‘  ) is dominated by the size of рқ‘  . The size of рқ‘  is given in Proposition 3.8, such that the parallelization (рқ‘қ в—Ұ рқ‘  рқ‘ҹ 2 , рқ‘  ) has size

S вүІ рқ‘Ғ log рқ‘Ғ + log рқңҖ вҲ’1 , B вүІ Poly( рқ‘Ғ ).

By the choice of рқ‘ҷ 1, the size of рқңҷ mult is of the same order as the ones of рқ‘қ в—Ұ рқ‘  рқ‘ҹ 2 and рқ‘қ в—Ұ рқ‘  рқ‘ҹ 2 , which are dominated by the size of (рқ‘қ в—Ұ рқ‘  рқ‘ҹ 2 , рқ‘  ) and (рқ‘қ в—Ұ рқ‘  рқ‘ҹ 2 , рқ‘  ). Thus, рқңҷ mult (рқ‘қ в—Ұ рқ‘  рқ‘ҹ 2 , рқ‘  ) has the same asymptotic size as рқ‘  and рқңҷ mult (рқ‘қ в—Ұ рқ‘  рқ‘ҹ 2 , рқ‘  ) has the same size as (рқ‘қ в—Ұ рқ‘  рқ‘ҹ 2 , рқ‘  ) derived above. Finally, the sum of рқңҷ mult (рқ‘қ в—Ұ рқ‘  рқ‘ҹ 2 , рқ‘  )

and рқңҷ mult (рқ‘қ в—Ұ рқ‘  рқ‘ҹ 2 , рқ‘  ) giving the total network size evaluates as 

L вүІ (log рқ‘Ғ log log рқ‘Ғ + log 2 рқңҖ вҲ’1 ) вҲЁ (log рқ‘Ғ + log рқңҖ вҲ’1 ) W вүІ рқ‘Ғ log 2 рқ‘Ғ + рқ‘Ғ 

41 вүІ log рқ‘Ғ log log рқ‘Ғ + log 2 рқңҖ вҲ’1 , вүІ рқ‘Ғ log 2 рқ‘Ғ , 

S вүІ рқ‘Ғ log 3 рқ‘Ғ + log 2 рқңҖ вҲ’1 + рқ‘Ғ log рқ‘Ғ + log рқңҖ вҲ’1 B вүІ (рқ‘Ғ 1/( рқ‘‘ вҲ’1) вҲЁ рқңҖ вҲ’4 ) вҲЁ Poly( рқ‘Ғ )

вүІ рқ‘Ғ log 3 рқ‘Ғ + log 2 рқңҖ вҲ’1 , вүІ Poly( рқ‘Ғ ) вҲЁ рқңҖ вҲ’4 .

G. Basic results in neural network approximation theory 

Here we summarise some important known results on approximation properties of neural networks from the class NN( L, W, S, B) introduced in (2.15). 

Lemma G.1 (composition of neural networks) . Let рқңҷ 1 вҲҲ NN( L1, W1, S1, B1) and рқңҷ 2 вҲҲ NN( L2, W2, S2, B2).Then its composition рқңҷ = рқңҷ 1 в—Ұ рқңҷ 2 has size 

L вүӨ L1 + L2 + 1 , W = W1 вҲЁ W2, S = S1 + S2, B = B1 вҲЁ B2.

Lemma G.2 (parallelisation of neural networks, [24] Lemma F.3) . Let рқңҷ 1 вҲҲ NN( L1, W1, S1, B1) and рқңҷ 2 вҲҲNN( L2, W2, S2, B2). Then the parallelisation рқңҷ = ( рқңҷ 1, рқңҷ 2) has size 

L вүӨ L1 вҲЁ L2, W = 2( W1 + W2), S = 2( S1 + LW 1 + S2 + LW 2), B = B1 вҲЁ B2.

Lemma G.3 (sum of neural networks, [24] Lemma F.3) . Let рқңҷ рқ‘– вҲҲ NN( Li, Wi, Si, Bi), рқ‘– = 1 , вҖҰ , рқ‘ҡ . Then the sum рқңҷ = вҲ‘рқ‘ҡ рқ‘– =1 рқңҷ рқ‘– is a neural network with size

max( рқ‘Ҙ, рқ‘Ұ ) = рқ‘Ұ + ReLU( рқ‘Ҙ вҲ’ рқ‘Ұ ), min( рқ‘Ҙ, рқ‘Ұ ) = рқ‘Ҙ вҲ’ ReLU( рқ‘Ҙ вҲ’ рқ‘Ұ ), рқ‘Ҙ, рқ‘Ұ вҲҲ в„қ.

Lemma G.8 (growth control of neural network) . For рқ‘ҡ вҲҲ в„•, there exists a neural network рқңҷ cap вҲҲ NN(( рқ‘ҡ +1) log( рқ‘ҡ + 1) , рқ‘ҡ + 1 , (рқ‘ҡ + 1) log( рқ‘ҡ + 1) , 2рқ‘ҡ +1 ) such that

Proof. The proof is a simpler version of [14, Lemma 3.12]. Invoking Lemma G.7 with рқ‘ҷ 2 = 1 , рқ‘ҳ = рқ‘ҡ , and 

рқ‘ҳ = 0 , there exists a neural network Мғ рқңҷ cap such that 

|рқңҷ rec (рқ‘Ҙ ) вҲ’ рқ‘Ҙ вҲ’1 | вүӨ 12 вүӨ 12рқ‘Ҙ , рқ‘Ҙ вҲҲ [2 вҲ’рқ‘ҡ , 1] 

вҮ’ 12рқ‘Ҙ = 1

рқ‘Ҙ вҲ’ 12рқ‘Ҙ вүӨМғ рқңҷ cap (рқ‘Ҙ ) вүӨ 1

рқ‘Ҙ + 12рқ‘Ҙ = 32рқ‘Ҙ .

Multiplying with 4( рқ‘‘ + 2) , setting рқ‘ҹ = 1 вҲ’ рқ‘Ҙ , and рқңҷ cap (рқ‘ҹ ) вү” 4( рқ‘‘ + 2) Мғрқңҷ cap (1 вҲ’ рқ‘ҹ ) yields the claim. в–
